<p><b>Herbert Clark Hoover</b> (August 10, 1874 – October 20, 1964) was an American politician who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">31st President of the United States</a> from 1929 to 1933 during the Great Depression. A Republican, as Secretary of Commerce in the 1920s he introduced <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a> themes of efficiency in the business community and provided government support for standardization, efficiency and international trade. As president from 1929 to 1933, his ambitious programs were overwhelmed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, which seemed to get worse every year despite the increasingly large-scale interventions he made in the economy. He was defeated in a landslide in 1932 by Democrat <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, and spent the rest of his life as a conservative denouncing big government, liberalism and federal intervention in economic affairs, as Democrats repeatedly used his Depression record to attack conservatism and justify more regulation of the economy.</p>
<p>A lifelong <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a>, he became a successful <a href="/wiki/Mining_engineer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining engineer">mining engineer</a> around the globe and retired in 1912. In the First World War he built an international reputation as a humanitarian by leading relief efforts in Belgium during the war, and in Eastern Europe afterwards. He headed the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Food Administration">U.S. Food Administration</a> during World War I. His reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive businessman</a> fighting for efficiency and elimination of waste was built as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</a> 1921-28. Hoover was a leader in the <a href="/wiki/Efficiency_Movement" title="Efficiency Movement">Efficiency Movement</a>, which held that every institution public and private was riddled with unsuspected inefficiencies. They all could be improved by experts who could identify the problems and solve them. He also believed in the importance of volunteerism and of the role of individuals in society and the economy. In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">the presidential election of 1928</a>, Hoover easily won the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Republican_Party" title="History of the United States Republican Party">Republican nomination</a>, despite having no elected-office experience. Although Hoover never raised the religious issue, some of his supporters did in mobilizing anti-Catholic sentiment against his opponent <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith" title="Al Smith">Al Smith</a>. Hoover won in a landslide.</p>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">Wall Street Crash of 1929</a> struck less than eight months after he took office, Hoover tried to combat the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a> with large-scale government public works projects such as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a>, and calls on industry to keep wages high. He reluctantly approved the <a href="/wiki/Smoot%E2%80%93Hawley_Tariff" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoot–Hawley Tariff">Smoot–Hawley Tariff</a> of 1930, which sent foreign trade spiralling down. He believed it was essential to balance the budget despite falling tax revenue, so he raised the tax rates. The economy kept falling, and the unemployment rate rose to 25%, with heavy industry, mining, and wheat and cotton farming hit especially hard. This downward spiral, plus his support for prohibition policies that had lost favor, set the stage for Hoover's overwhelming defeat in 1932 by Democrat <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, who promised a <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. Most historians agree that Hoover's defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">1932 election</a> was caused primarily by the downward economic spiral, although his strong support for <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibition</a> was also significant.</p>
<p>Hoover became a conservative spokesman in opposition to the domestic and foreign policies of the New Deal. He opposed entry into the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> and was not called on to serve in any public role during the war. He had better relations with President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, and Hoover helped produce <a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Economic_Mission_to_Germany_and_Austria" title="The President's Economic Mission to Germany and Austria">a number of reports</a> that changed U.S. occupation policy in Germany. Truman also appointed Hoover to head the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Commission" title="Hoover Commission">Hoover Commission</a>, intended to foster greater efficiency throughout the federal bureaucracy, and Hoover served on a similar commission under President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>. By the time of his death in 1964, he had rehabilitated his image. Nevertheless, Hoover is generally not ranked highly in <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">historical rankings of Presidents of the United States</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Presidency_.281929.E2.80.931933.29"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Presidency (1929–1933)</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background_and_early_life">Family background and early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family background and early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/130px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" width="130" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/195px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg/260px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg 2x" data-file-width="334" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1877.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1877 Herbert Hoover tintype</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Herbert Hoover was born on August 10, 1874, in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>; he would become the only President so far born in that state and the first born west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. His father, Jesse Hoover (1849–80), was a <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">blacksmith</a> and farm implement store owner, of German (Pfautz, Wehmeyer), German-Swiss (Huber, Burkhart), and British ancestry. Jesse Hoover and his father Eli had moved to Iowa from <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> twenty years previously.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner19964_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner19964-1">[1]</a></sup> Hoover's mother, Hulda Randall Minthorn (1849–84), was born in <a href="/wiki/Norwich,_Ontario" title="Norwich, Ontario">Norwich, Ontario</a>, Canada, and was of English and Irish ancestry. Both of his parents were <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quakers</a>.</p>
<p>At about age two he contracted the <a href="/wiki/Croup" title="Croup">croup</a>. He was so ill that he was momentarily thought to have died, until he was resuscitated by his uncle, John Minthorn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner19966_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner19966-2">[2]</a></sup> As a child, he was often called by his father "my little stick in the mud", since he repeatedly was trapped in the mud while crossing an unpaved street.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner19967_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner19967-3">[3]</a></sup> Hoover's family figured prominently in the town's public prayer life, due almost entirely to Hulda's role in her church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner19969_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner19969-4">[4]</a></sup> His father, noted by the local paper for his "pleasant, sunshiny disposition", died in 1880. After working to retire her husband's debts, retain their life insurance, and care for the children, his mother died in 1884, leaving Hoover (age nine), his older brother, and his younger sister as orphans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199610_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199610-5">[5]</a></sup> Fellow Quaker <a href="/wiki/Lawrie_Tatum" title="Lawrie Tatum">Lawrie Tatum</a> was appointed as Hoover's guardian.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/220px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/330px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg/440px-Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_birthplace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover birthplace cottage, West Branch, Iowa</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After a brief stay with one of his grandmothers in <a href="/wiki/Kingsley,_Iowa" title="Kingsley, Iowa">Kingsley, Iowa</a>, Hoover lived the next 18 months with his uncle Allen Hoover in West Branch. In November 1885, he went to <a href="/wiki/Newberg,_Oregon" title="Newberg, Oregon">Newberg, Oregon</a>, to live with his uncle Dr. John Minthorn, a physician and businessman whose own son had died the year before. The Minthorn household was considered cultured and educational, and imparted a strong work ethic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199612_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199612-6">[6]</a></sup> Observers, including Minthorn himself, describe Hoover as being unhappy with the long days of toil he experienced while staying with the Minthorn household. Hoover attended Friends Pacific Academy (now <a href="/wiki/George_Fox_University" title="George Fox University">George Fox University</a>), but dropped out at the age of thirteen to become an office assistant for his uncle's real estate office in <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Oregon" title="Salem, Oregon">Salem, Oregon</a>. Though he did not attend high school, Hoover attended night school and learned bookkeeping, typing, and mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_20094-6_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_20094-6-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover entered <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> in 1891, its inaugural year, after failing all the entrance exams (except mathematics) and then being tutored for the summer in Palo Alto.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199616_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199616-8">[8]</a></sup> Hoover claimed to be the very first student at Stanford, by virtue of having been the first person in the first class to sleep in the dormitory.<sup id="cite_ref-e1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e1-9">[9]</a></sup> Hoover worked a variety of odd jobs to support himself, and struggled in many of his classes, especially English. But he found a happiness at Stanford that previously eluded him. Hoover had been unsure of his major before arriving at Stanford, but position working for geologist <a href="/wiki/John_Casper_Branner" title="John Casper Branner">John Casper Branner</a> convinced him to switch his major to <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, and Hoover interned for Branner and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> during the summer. Though he was shy among fellow students at first, Hoover won election as student treasurer and became known for his distaste for <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities" title="Fraternities and sororities">fraternities and sororities</a>. In his senior year, he became smitten with a classmate named <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Henry</a>, but his financial situation precluded the possibility of marriage. Hoover graduated from Stanford in 1895, in the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a>, and initially struggled to find a job.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_20096-9_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_20096-9-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>While at the university, he was the student manager of both the baseball and football teams and was a part of the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Big_Game_(American_football)" title="Big Game (American football)">Big Game</a> versus rival the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California</a> and friend (Cal Manager) Herbert Lang.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Only 10,000 tickets were printed for the inaugural game and 20,000 people showed up. Both Hoover and Lang had to find pots, bowls and any other available receptacles to collect admission fees.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Stanford won the game.<sup id="cite_ref-e1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e1-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:21–22</sup> In 1892 Hoover invited Polish composer <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_Jan_Paderewski" title="Ignacy Jan Paderewski">Ignacy Jan Paderewski</a> to give a benefit concert. Hoover and his associates were unable to pay Paderewski the entire honorarium. The musician after hearing their story returned them the money so they could pay for rental of the concert hall. In 1919 Paderewski, who become prime minister of <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, traveled to Hoover to thank him for the relief sent to Poland. "That's all right, Mr. Paderewski," Hoover replied. "Besides, you don't remember it, but you helped me once when I was a student at college and I was in a hole."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining_engineer">Mining engineer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mining engineer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg/150px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" width="150" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg/225px-Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg 2x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="414" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_in_1898.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover, aged 23; taken in <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, Western Australia, in 1898</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After graduation, Hoover worked in the gold mining districts of <a href="/wiki/Nevada_City,_California" title="Nevada City, California">Nevada City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grass_Valley,_California" title="Grass Valley, California">Grass Valley, California</a>, before landing a job with the mining engineering firm of Louis Janin.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:25–28</sup> Hoover went to <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> in 1897 as an employee of Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co., a <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>-based gold mining company. His geological training and work experience were well suited for the firm's objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199624.E2.80.9326_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199624.E2.80.9326-17">[17]</a></sup> He worked at gold mines in <a href="/wiki/Big_Bell,_Western_Australia" title="Big Bell, Western Australia">Big Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cue,_Western_Australia" title="Cue, Western Australia">Cue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gwalia,_Western_Australia" title="Gwalia, Western Australia">Gwalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Menzies,_Western_Australia" title="Menzies, Western Australia">Menzies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coolgardie,_Western_Australia" title="Coolgardie, Western Australia">Coolgardie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover first went to Coolgardie, then the center of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Goldfields" title="Eastern Goldfields">Eastern Goldfields</a>, where he worked under Edward Hooper, a company partner. Conditions were harsh in these goldfields even though he got a $5,000 salary (equivalent to $100,000 today).<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_biography_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover_biography-20">[20]</a></sup> In the Coolgardie and Murchison <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangelands</a> on the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Victoria_Desert" title="Great Victoria Desert">Great Victoria Desert</a>, Hoover described the region as a land of "black flies, red dust and white heat".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> He served as a geologist and mining engineer while searching the Western Australian goldfields for investments. After being appointed as mine manager at the age of 23, he led a major program of expansion for the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Gwalia" title="Sons of Gwalia">Sons of Gwalia</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold mine">gold mine</a> at Gwalia,<sup id="cite_ref-Blainey_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blainey-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:202</sup> and brought in many <a href="/wiki/Italian_immigrants" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian immigrants">Italian immigrants</a> to cut costs and counter the <a href="/wiki/Australian_labour_movement" title="Australian labour movement">labour movement</a> of the Australian miners.<sup id="cite_ref-HG_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HG-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Hoover became opposed to measures such as the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_compensation" title="Workers' compensation">workers' compensation</a>, feeling that they were unfair to owners. In 1898, Hoover was promoted to junior partner by his employers, who were pleased with Hoover's talent and devotion to his work. After earning his promotion, he cabled his college sweetheart, Lou Henry, asking her to marry him. After she cabled back her acceptance of the proposal, Hoover briefly returned to the United States to marry her. Hoover and his wife would have two children: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Jr." title="Herbert Hoover Jr.">Herbert Charles Hoover</a> (1903–1969) and <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hoover" title="Allan Hoover">Allan Henry Hoover</a> (1907–1993).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200910-13_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200910-13-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rather than returning to Australia, Hoover and his new wife traveled to China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200910-13_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200910-13-25">[25]</a></sup> An open feud had developed between Hoover and his boss Ernest Williams, with Hoover persuading four other mine managers to conspire against his rival. Defusing the situation, the firm's principals offered Hoover a compelling promotion that relocated him to China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199632_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199632-26">[26]</a></sup> During his time at Gwalia, Hoover first met Fleury James Lyster, a pioneering <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairweather_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairweather-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In Western Australia friends called Hoover "H.C." or the old nickname "Hail Columbia".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983292.E2.80.93293_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983292.E2.80.93293-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China_and_other_global_operations">China and other global operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: China and other global operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover's work in China revolved around the huge <a href="/wiki/Kaiping_Mines" title="Kaiping Mines">Kaiping Mines</a>. Hoover worked as chief engineer for the Chinese Bureau of Mines, and as general manager for the Chinese Engineering and Mining Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Later he worked for Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co. as the company's lead engineer. Hoover's wife learned <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Mandarin Chinese</a> (she was a first-rate linguist), and he also learned some of the language while he worked in China; it is said that they used it during his tenure at the White House when they wanted to foil eavesdroppers.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Hoover made recommendations to improve the lot of the Chinese worker, seeking to end the practice of imposing long term servitude contracts and to institute reforms for workers based on merit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199634_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199634-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> trapped the Hoovers in <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> in June 1900. For almost a month, the settlement was under fire, and both dedicated themselves to defense of their city. Hoover himself guided U.S. Marines around Tianjin during the battle, using his knowledge of the local terrain.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Mrs. Hoover meanwhile devoted her efforts at the various hospitals and even wielded, and willingly and accurately deployed, a .38-caliber pistol.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199637_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199637-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg/300px-Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" width="300" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg/450px-Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/57/Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lou.henry.on.a.burro.at.acton.CA.1891.08.22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lou Henry, age 17, on a burro and rifle-ready at <a href="/wiki/Acton,_California" title="Acton, California">Acton, California</a> on August 22, 1891</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover was made a partner in Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co. on December 18, 1901 and assumed responsibility for various Australian operations and investments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash_1983224_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash_1983224-35">[35]</a></sup> His initial compensation rose to $12,500 annually in addition to a 20% share of profits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199638_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199638-36">[36]</a></sup> The company eventually controlled at one point approximately 50% of gold production in Western Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983283_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983283-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1901, Hoover no longer lived in Australia, but he visited the country in 1902, 1903, 1905, and 1907 as an overseas investor. Hoover was also a director of Chinese Engineering and Mining Corporation (CEMC) when it became a supplier of immigrant labor from Southeast Asia for <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> mines.<sup id="cite_ref-Liggett_1930_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liggett_1930-38">[38]</a></sup> The first shipment of almost 2,000 workers arrived in Durban from Qinhuangdao in July 1904. By 1906, the total number of immigrant workers increased to 50,000, almost entirely recruited and shipped by CEMC. When the living and working conditions of the laborers became known, public opposition to the scheme grew and questions were asked in the British Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The scheme was abandoned in 1911.</p>
<p>In August–September 1905, he founded the <a href="/wiki/Zinc_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc Corporation">Zinc Corporation</a> (eventually part of the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Tinto_Group" title="Rio Tinto Group">Rio Tinto Group</a>) with <a href="/wiki/William_Baillieu" title="William Baillieu">William Baillieu</a> and others. The lead-silver ore produced at <a href="/wiki/Broken_Hill,_New_South_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Broken Hill, New South Wales">Broken Hill, New South Wales</a> was rich in <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. But the zinc could not be recovered due to "the Sulphide Problem", and was left in the tailings that remained after the silver and lead was extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-Blainey_1968a_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blainey_1968a-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Zinc Corporation proposed to buy the tailings and extract the zinc by a new process. The <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> process was then being developed at Broken Hill, although the Zinc Corporation struggled to apply it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199624.E2.80.9343_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199624.E2.80.9343-41">[41]</a></sup> Hoover was in Australia in 1905. F.J. Lyster, originally a carpenter before becoming a foreman in the gravity mill, perfected the "Lyster Process",<sup id="cite_ref-Fairweather_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairweather-27">[27]</a></sup> which enabled the Zinc Corporation to operate the world's first selective or differential flotation plant, According the <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Blainey" title="Geoffrey Blainey">Geoffrey Blainey</a>, though the process was not fully understood, a patent was applied for in May 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-Blainey_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blainey-22">[22]</a></sup> Hoover's brother, Theodore J. Hoover, also came to Broken Hill.</p>
<p>"Broken Hill was one of the dreariest places in the world at this time. It lay in the middle of the desert, was unbelievably hot in summer, had no fresh water, no vegetation, and mountains of tailings blew into every crack with every wisp of wind."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Despite these miserable conditions, Hoover and his associates became suppliers to world industry of zinc and other vital base minerals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sole_proprietor">Sole proprietor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sole proprietor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HHoover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/170px-HHoover.jpg" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/255px-HHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HHoover.jpg/340px-HHoover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="918" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HHoover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover in his 30s while a mining engineer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1908, Hoover became an independent mining consultant, traveling worldwide until the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in 1914. He left Bewick, Moreing &amp; Co and, setting out on his own, eventually ended up with investments on every continent and offices in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg,_Florida" title="St. Petersburg, Florida">St. Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandalay" title="Mandalay">Mandalay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> He specialized in rejuvenating troubled mining operations, taking a share of the profits in exchange for his technical and financial expertise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200911-13_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200911-13-44">[44]</a></sup> He had his second successful venture with the British firm Burma Corporation, again producing silver, lead, and zinc in large quantities at the <a href="/wiki/Namtu" title="Namtu">Namtu</a> Bawdwin Mine, where he caught malaria in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:90–96,101–102</sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983381_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983381-45">[45]</a></sup> He also helped increase copper production in <a href="/wiki/Kyshtym" title="Kyshtym">Kyshtym</a>, Russia, through the use of pyritic smelting. Then he agreed to manage one of the Russian Czar's Cabinet Mines located in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>. The oxidized lead-zinc-silver ore contained copper and gold as well. According to Hoover, "It developed probably the greatest and richest single body of ore known in the world" before the Communist Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:102–108</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kennan_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennan-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>While living in London, noting the American engineer's patriotic intensity, some British acquaintances referred to him as the "star-spangled Hoover". It recalled the nickname he had acquired in the Australian outback: "Hail Columbia" Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983502_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983502-47">[47]</a></sup> The Bawdwin mine ultimately became the chief source of Hoover's fortune.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199643_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199643-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his spare time, Hoover wrote. His lectures at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia</a> and Stanford universities were published in 1909 as <i>Principles of Mining</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> which became a standard textbook. The book reflects his move towards <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">Progressive</a> ideals, as Hoover came to endorse eight-hour workdays and organized labor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200918-20_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200918-20-50">[50]</a></sup> Hoover and his wife also published their English translation of the 1556 mining classic <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_metallica" title="De re metallica">De re metallica</a></i> in 1912. This translation from the Latin of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> author <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a> is still the most important scholarly version and provides its historical context.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgricola1912_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgricola1912-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1914, Hoover was a very wealthy man, with an estimated personal fortune of $4 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983569_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983569-52">[52]</a></sup> He was once quoted as saying "If a man has not made a million dollars by the time he is forty, he is not worth much".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983509_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983509-53">[53]</a></sup> By 1914, Hoover stood eventually to obtain what he later described as "a large fortune from these Russian industries, probably more than is good for anybody".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983446_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983446-54">[54]</a></sup> Sixty-six years after opening the mine in 1897, Hoover still had a partial share in the <a href="/wiki/Sons_of_Gwalia" title="Sons of Gwalia">Sons of Gwalia</a> mine when it finally closed in 1963, just one year before the former President's death in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in 1964. The successful mine had yielded $55m in gold and $10m in dividends for investors.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoover_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover-13">[13]</a></sup> Herbert Hoover, acting as a main investor, financier, mining speculator, and organizer of men, played a major role in the important metallurgical developments that occurred in Broken Hill in the first decade of the twentieth century, developments that had a great impact on the mining and production of silver, lead, and zinc.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983411_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983411-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In later years Hoover thought of himself and his associates as "engineering doctors to sick concerns", hence his reputation as the "Doctor of sick mines".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENash1983392_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENash1983392-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanitarian_work">Humanitarian work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Humanitarian work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relief_in_Europe_and_Belgium">Relief in Europe and Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Relief in Europe and Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Wanted_Immediately._2,000,000_Garments_for_destitute_Men,_Women,_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/170px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/255px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg/340px-%22Wanted_Immediately._2%2C000%2C000_Garments_for_destitute_Men%2C_Women%2C_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2131" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%22Wanted_Immediately._2,000,000_Garments_for_destitute_Men,_Women,_and_children_in_occupied_Northern_France_and..._-_NARA_-_512616.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster for the <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a> for Belgium directed by Hoover.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> began in August 1914, Hoover helped organize the return of around 120,000 Americans from Europe. He led 500 volunteers in distributing food, clothing, steamship tickets and cash.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> "I did not realize it at the moment, but on August 3, 1914, my career was over forever. I was on the slippery road of public life."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Hoover liked to say that the difference between dictatorship and democracy was simple: dictators organize from the top down, democracies from the bottom up.</p>
<p>When Belgium faced a food crisis after being invaded by Germany in 1914, Hoover undertook an unprecedented relief effort with the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Relief_in_Belgium" title="Commission for Relief in Belgium">Commission for Relief in Belgium</a> (CRB).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199674_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199674-64">[64]</a></sup> As chairman of the CRB, Hoover worked with the leader of the Belgian <i><a href="/wiki/Comit%C3%A9_National_de_Secours_et_d%27Alimentation" title="Comité National de Secours et d'Alimentation">Comité National de Secours et d'Alimentation</a></i> (CNSA), <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Francqui" title="Émile Francqui">Émile Francqui</a>, to feed the entire nation for the duration of the war. The CRB obtained and imported millions of tons of foodstuffs for the CNSA to distribute, and watched over the CNSA to make sure the German army did not appropriate the food. The CRB became a veritable independent republic of relief, with its own flag, navy, factories, mills, and railroads. Private donations and government grants (78%) supplied an $11-million-a-month budget.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199679_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199679-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the next two years, Hoover worked 14-hour days from London, administering the distribution of over two million tons of food to nine million war victims. In an early form of <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_diplomacy" title="Shuttle diplomacy">shuttle diplomacy</a>, he crossed the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> forty times to meet with German authorities and persuade them to allow food shipments, becoming an international hero. The Belgian city of <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Leuven</a> named a prominent square <i>Hooverplein</i> after him. At its peak, Hoover's <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a> (ARA) fed 10.5 million people daily. Great Britain grew reluctant to support the CRB, preferring instead to emphasize Germany's obligation to supply the relief; <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, whom Hoover intensely disliked, led a military faction that considered the Belgian relief effort "a positive military disaster".</p>
<p>During this time, Hoover made a strong impression on the American Ambassador to Great Britain, Walter Page. In a Memoranda dated December 30, 1916, Page wrote: Mr. Herbert C. Hoover, Chairman of the Commission for Relief in Belgium, would, if opportunity should offer, make a useful officer in the State Department. He is probably the only man living who has privately (i.e., without holding office) negotiated understandings with the British, French, German, Dutch, and Belgian governments. He personally knows and has had direct dealings with these governments, and his transactions with them have involved several hundred million dollars. He is a man of very considerable fortune—less than when the war began, for tins relief work has cost him much. He was approached on behalf of the British Government with the suggestion that if he would become a British subject the Government would be pleased to give him an important executive post and with the hint that if he succeeded a title might await him. His answer was: "I'll do what I can for you with pleasure; but I'll be damned if I'll give up my American citizenship—not on your life!" Within the last six months two large financial organizations, each independently, have offered him $100,000 a year to enter their service; and an industrial company offered him $100,000 "to start with." He declined them all. When the Belgian relief work recently struck a financial snag, Hoover by telegraph got the promise of a loan in the United States to the British and French governments for Belgian relief of $150,000,000.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Food_Administration">U.S. Food Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: U.S. Food Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the United States entered the war in April 1917, President Woodrow Wilson appointed Hoover to head the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Food_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Food Administration">U.S. Food Administration</a>, which was created under the Lever Food Control Act in 1917 to ensure the nation's food needs during the war. This was a position he actively sought, though he later claimed it was thrust upon him. He was convinced from his Belgian work that centralization of authority was essential to any relief effort; he demanded, and got, great power albeit not as much as he sought.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner199696.E2.80.9397_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner199696.E2.80.9397-66">[66]</a></sup> Hoover believed "food will win the war"; and beginning on September 29, this slogan was introduced and put into frequent use.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner1996102_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner1996102-67">[67]</a></sup> Earning the appellation of "food czar," Hoover recruited a volunteer force of hundreds of thousands of women and deployed <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_in_World_War_I" title="Propaganda in World War I">propaganda</a> in movie theaters, schools, and churches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200941-43_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200941-43-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>He carefully selected men to assist in the agency leadership – Alonzo Taylor (technical abilities), <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert Taft</a> (political associations), <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a> (agricultural influence) and Julius Barnes (business acumen).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner1996101_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner1996101-69">[69]</a></sup> Determined to avoid rationing, Hoover established set days for people to avoid eating specified foods and save them for soldiers' rations: meatless Mondays, wheatless Wednesdays, and "when in doubt, eat potatoes." These policies were dubbed "Hooverizing" by government publicists, in spite of Hoover's continual orders that publicity should not mention him by name. The agency employed a system of price controls and licensing requirements for suppliers to maximize production. Despite efforts to prevent it, some companies reaped great profits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurner1996104.E2.80.93109_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurner1996104.E2.80.93109-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_relief_and_1920_election">Post-war relief and 1920 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Post-war relief and 1920 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Days after the end of World War I in November 1918, Hoover sailed to Europe. The United States Food Administration became the <a href="/wiki/American_Relief_Administration" title="American Relief Administration">American Relief Administration</a> (ARA), and Hoover was charged with providing food to Central and Eastern Europe. The ARA fed millions, including the inhabitants of Germany and the former <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg Empire">Habsburg Empire</a>. U.S. government funding for the ARA expired in the summer of 1919, and Hoover transformed the ARA into a private organization, raising millions of dollars from private donors. Under the auspices of the ARA, the European Children's Fund fed millions of starving children. In addition to nourishing millions, the ARA also helped the United States avoid a potentially problematic domestic food surplus. In response to criticism over his willingness to grant aid to countries under the sway of Bolshevism, Hoover stated, "Twenty million people are starving. Whatever their politics, they shall be fed!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200941-43.2C_57-58_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200941-43.2C_57-58-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reflecting the gratitude of many Europeans, in July 1922, Soviet author <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a> wrote to Hoover:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Your help will enter history as a unique, gigantic achievement, worthy of the greatest glory, which will long remain in the memory of millions of Russians whom you have saved from death.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As one of the most powerful individuals in Europe, Hoover became involved in continental politics. He was broadly supportive of Wilson's <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a>, and urged ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>. He opposed a <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_Austria%27s_attempts_to_retake_the_throne_of_Hungary" title="Charles I of Austria's attempts to retake the throne of Hungary">monarchist coup</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and demanded the appointment of the moderate <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_Jan_Paderewski" title="Ignacy Jan Paderewski">Ignacy Jan Paderewski</a> in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>. He condemned <a href="/wiki/Bolshevism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevism">Bolshevism</a>, but warned President Wilson against an <a href="/wiki/Allied_intervention_in_the_Russian_Civil_War" title="Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War">intervention</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, viewing the <a href="/wiki/White_movement" title="White movement">White Russian</a> forces as little better and fearing the possibility of a protracted U.S. involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200943-45_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200943-45-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover had been little-known among the American public before 1914, but emerged as the perhaps the second-most famous person in the United States after President Wilson. In the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1920" title="United States presidential election, 1920">1920 presidential election</a>, Hoover was often mentioned as a potential candidate, but his partisan affiliation was unclear. Hoover particularly appealed to progressives of both parties, who commended his war-time push for higher taxes, criticism of Attorney General <a href="/wiki/A._Mitchell_Palmer" title="A. Mitchell Palmer">A. Mitchell Palmer</a>, and advocacy for measures such as the minimum wage, forty-eight-hour workweek, and elimination of <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">child labor</a>. Yet Hoover's <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Alger_myth" title="Horatio Alger myth">"rags to riches"</a> story and war-time leadership made him appealing to many others as well. In March 1920, Hoover publicly declared his allegiance with the GOP, stating that he would not run for but would not refuse the 1920 Republican nomination. Hoover had various reasons for choosing the Republican Party, including a split with Wilson over the Versailles Treaty (Hoover had come to accept the <a href="/wiki/Lodge_Reservations" title="Lodge Reservations">Lodge Reservations</a> to the treaty), and his view that the Democrats would likely lose the 1920 election. However, the conservative Old Guard of the GOP viewed Hoover warily, and his time as Food Czar had also made him numerous enemies among farmers, an important bloc in the GOP. Hoover's candidacy for the GOP nomination fizzled out after his defeat in the California primary by <a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite son</a> <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Johnson" title="Hiram Johnson">Hiram Johnson</a>. In the general election, Hoover supported the Republican nominee, <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a>, who emerged victorious.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200945-50_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200945-50-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1919, Hoover established the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution_Library_and_Archive" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoover Institution Library and Archive">Hoover War Collection</a> at Stanford University. He donated all the files of the Commission for Relief in Belgium, the U.S. Food Administration, and the American Relief Administration, and pledged $50,000 as an endowment. Scholars were sent to Europe to collect pamphlets, society publications, government documents, newspapers, posters, proclamations, and other ephemeral materials related to the war and the revolutions that followed it. The collection was later renamed the Hoover War Library and is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After working with the Food Administration, Hoover became president of the Federated American Engineering Societies.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Himmelberg_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Himmelberg-76">[76]</a></sup> Hoover's design was set on increasing efficiency and reducing waste.<sup id="cite_ref-Himmelberg_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Himmelberg-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> As president of the FAES, Hoover constructed a plan to study waste in the industrial sector, and he had a strong focus on labor matters.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The result was a 400-page report titled <i>Waste in Industry</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> which was a highly publicized report at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Hoover became Commerce Secretary, he resigned from the FAES.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The FAES report <i>Waste in Industry</i>, however, served as a blueprint for the Department of Commerce for the next several years.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Secretary of Commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HooverCommerce1926.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/HooverCommerce1926.jpg/250px-HooverCommerce1926.jpg" width="250" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/HooverCommerce1926.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="294" data-file-height="190" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HooverCommerce1926.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assistants William McCracken (left) &amp; Walter Drake (right) with Secretary Hoover (center)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After his election as president in 1920, Harding rewarded Hoover for his support, offering to appoint him as either <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> or <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</a>. Expecting opposition from the Republican-controlled Senate, Hoover initially decided to refuse a Cabinet position, but Harding paired Hoover's nomination with that of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mellon" title="Andrew Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>, who many Republicans hoped would become <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a>. Secretary of Commerce was considered a minor Cabinet post, with limited and vaguely defined responsibilities, but Hoover decided to accept the position. Hoover would remain in office until 1928, serving in both the Harding and Coolidge administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200951-52_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200951-52-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover envisioned the Commerce Department as the hub of the nation's growth and stability. From Harding he demanded, and received, authority to coordinate economic affairs throughout the government. He created many sub-departments and committees, overseeing and regulating everything from manufacturing statistics, the census and radio, to air travel. In some instances he "seized" control of responsibilities from other Cabinet departments when he deemed that they were not carrying out their responsibilities well. He became known as the "Secretary of Commerce and Under-Secretary of all other departments."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200953-63_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200953-63-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of Hoover's efforts as Commerce Secretary centered on eliminating waste and increasing efficiency in business and industry. This included reducing labor losses from trade disputes and seasonal fluctuations, reducing industrial losses from accident and injury, and reducing the amount of crude oil spilled during extraction and shipping. One major achievement was to promote product standardizations. He promoted international trade by opening overseas offices to advise businessmen. Hoover was especially eager to promote Hollywood films overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart1998_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart1998-86">[86]</a></sup> His "Own Your Own Home" campaign was a collaboration to promote ownership of single-family dwellings, with groups such as the Better Houses in America movement, the Architects' Small House Service Bureau, and the Home Modernizing Bureau. He worked with bankers and the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan" class="mw-redirect" title="Savings and loan">savings and loan</a> industry to promote the new long-term home mortgage, which dramatically stimulated home construction.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg/220px-Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="237" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_and_Harding_at_baseball_game.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover with President Harding at a baseball game in 1921.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover's detractors wondered why he did not do anything to reapportion congress after the <a href="/wiki/1920_United_States_Census" title="1920 United States Census">1920 United States Census</a> which saw an increase in urban and immigrant populations. The 1920 Census was the first and only Decennial Census where the results were not used to reapportion Congress; which ultimately influenced the 1928 Electoral College and impacted the Presidential Election.<sup id="cite_ref-slayton_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slayton-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-finan_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finan-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>As secretary and later as President, Hoover revolutionized relations between business and government. Rejecting the adversarial stance of Theodore Roosevelt, William Howard Taft, and Woodrow Wilson, he sought to make the Commerce Department a powerful service organization, empowered to forge cooperative voluntary partnerships between government and business. This philosophy is often called "<a href="/wiki/Associationalism" title="Associationalism">associationalism</a>". Both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">U.S. Department of Justice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> opposed Hoover's goals,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> ruled in <i>Maple Flooring Manufacturers' Assn. v. United States 268 U.S. 563 (1925)</i> that Hoover's policies served the public interest by producing "fairer price levels" and "avoid[ing] waste."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_and_air_travel">Radio and air travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Radio and air travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/170px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/255px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/HerbertClarkHoover.jpg/340px-HerbertClarkHoover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HerbertClarkHoover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover listening to a <a href="/wiki/Radio_receiver" title="Radio receiver">radio receiver</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover's radio conferences played a key role in the early organization, development and regulation of radio broadcasting. Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Act_of_1927" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Act of 1927">Radio Act of 1927</a>, the Secretary of Commerce was unable to deny radio licensing or reassign broadcast frequencies. With help from supporters Senator Dill and Representative White, Hoover brought the issue of radio control to the Senate floor. Hoover fought for more power to control the proliferation of licensed radio stations (which in 1927, stood at 732 stations). With help from Dill and White, Hoover promoted the Dill-White Bill which eventually would become the Radio Act of 1927. This act allowed the government to intervene and abolish radio stations that were deemed "non-useful" to the public. Hoover's attempts at regulating radio were not supported by all Congressmen, and he received much opposition from the Senate and from radio station owners. However, Hoover's contributions to regulate radio in its infancy heavily influenced the modern radio system.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover was also influential in the early development of air travel. He sought to create a thriving private industry boosted by indirect government subsidies. He encouraged the development of emergency landing fields and required all runways to be equipped with lights and radio beams. He also encouraged farmers to make use of planes for <a href="/wiki/Aerial_application" title="Aerial application">crop dusting</a>. In light of his efforts, Washington, D.C. named its first airport <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Field" title="Hoover Field">Hoover Field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200953-54_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200953-54-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traffic_conferences">Traffic conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Traffic conferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Commerce Secretary, Hoover also hosted two national conferences on street traffic, in 1924 and 1926 (a third convened in 1930, during Hoover's presidency). Collectively the meetings were called the National Conference on Street and Highway Safety. Hoover's chief objective was to address the growing casualty toll of traffic accidents, but the scope grew and soon embraced motor vehicle standards, rules of the road, and urban traffic control. He left the invited interest groups to negotiate agreements among themselves, which were then presented for adoption by states and localities. Because automotive trade associations were the best organized, many of the positions taken by the conferences reflected their interests. The conferences issued a model Uniform Vehicle Code for adoption by the states, and a Model Municipal Traffic Ordinance for adoption by cities. Both were widely influential, promoting greater uniformity between jurisdictions and tending to promote the automobile's priority in city streets.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mississippi_flood">Mississippi flood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mississippi flood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a> broke the banks and <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a> of the lower Mississippi River in early 1927, resulting in flooding of millions of acres and leaving 1.5 million people displaced from their homes. Although such a disaster did not fall under the duties of the Commerce Department, the governors of six states along the Mississippi specifically asked for Herbert Hoover in the emergency. President Coolidge appointed Hoover to coordinate the response to the flood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200968-69_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200968-69-95">[95]</a></sup> Hoover personally crisscrossed the Mississippi Valley, giving speeches and coordinating the response. He established over one hundred tent cities and a fleet over six hundred vessels, and raised $17 million. In large part due to his leadership during the flood crisis, by 1928, Hoover had begun to overshadow President Coolidge himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200968-71_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200968-71-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The treatment of African-Americans during the disaster endangered Hoover's reputation as a humanitarian. Local officials brutalized black farmers and prevented them from leaving relief camps, aid intended for African-American <a href="/wiki/Sharecroppers" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharecroppers">sharecroppers</a> was often given instead to the landowners, and black men often were conscripted by locals into forced labor, sometimes at gun point.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Knowing the potential damage to his presidential hopes if this became public, Hoover struck a deal with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Russa_Moton" title="Robert Russa Moton">Robert Russa Moton</a>, the prominent <a href="/wiki/African-American" class="mw-redirect" title="African-American">African-American</a> successor to <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> as president of the <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuskegee Institute">Tuskegee Institute</a>. In exchange for keeping the sufferings of African-Americans quiet, Hoover promised unprecedented influence for African-Americans should he become president. Moton agreed, and following the accommodationist philosophy of Washington, he worked actively to conceal the information from the media.<sup id="cite_ref-pbs_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_election_of_1928">Presidential election of 1928</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Presidential election of 1928">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">United States presidential election, 1928</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_nomination">Republican nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Republican nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When President Calvin Coolidge announced in August 1927 that he would not seek a second full term of office in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">1928 presidential election</a>, Hoover became the leading Republican candidate, despite the fact Coolidge was lukewarm on Hoover, often deriding his ambitious and popular Commerce Secretary as "Wonder Boy".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Coolidge had been reluctant to choose Hoover as his successor; on one occasion he remarked that "for six years that man has given me unsolicited advice—all of it bad. I was particularly offended by his comment to 'shit or get off the pot'."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell1957195_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell1957195-100">[100]</a></sup> Even so, Coolidge had no desire to split the party by publicly opposing the popular Commerce Secretary's nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967390.E2.80.93391Wilson1975122.E2.80.93123_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy1967390.E2.80.93391Wilson1975122.E2.80.93123-101">[101]</a></sup> Prior to the <a href="/wiki/1928_Republican_National_Convention" title="1928 Republican National Convention">1928 Republican National Convention</a>, many wary Republican leaders cast about for an alternative candidate such as Treasury Secretary Andrew Mellon, former Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Hughes" title="Charles Evans Hughes">Charles Evans Hughes</a>, or Coolidge.<sup id="cite_ref-rusnak_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rusnak-102">[102]</a></sup> But no challenger emerged, and Hoover won the presidential nomination on the first ballot of the convention. The delegates considered nominating incumbent Vice President Charles Dawes to be Hoover's <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a>. But Coolidge (who hated Dawes) remarked that this would be "a personal affront" to him. The convention instead selected <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Curtis" title="Charles Curtis">Charles Curtis</a> of Kansas.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Hoover accepted the nomination at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Stadium" title="Stanford Stadium">Stanford Stadium</a>, telling a huge crowd that he would continue the policies of the Harding and Coolidge administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200971-72_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200971-72-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_election">General election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: General election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1928.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/ElectoralCollege1928.svg/275px-ElectoralCollege1928.svg.png" width="275" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/ElectoralCollege1928.svg/413px-ElectoralCollege1928.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/ElectoralCollege1928.svg/550px-ElectoralCollege1928.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1928.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1928 electoral vote results.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover campaigned for efficiency and the Republican record of prosperity against Democrat <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred E. Smith">Alfred E. Smith</a>. Smith likewise was a proponent of efficiency earned as governor of New York. Both candidates were pro-business, and each promised to improve conditions for farmers, reform immigration laws, and maintain America’s isolationist foreign policy. Where they differed was on the <a href="/wiki/Volstead_Act" title="Volstead Act">Volstead Act</a> which outlawed the sale of liquor and beer. Smith was a “wet” who called for its repeal, whereas Hoover gave limited support for prohibition, calling it an “experiment noble in purpose.” His use of <i>“experiment”</i> suggested it was not permanent. While Smith won extra support among Catholics in the big cities, he was also the target of intense <a href="/wiki/Anti-Catholicism" title="Anti-Catholicism">anti-Catholicism</a> from some Protestant communities, especially amongst Southern Baptists and German Lutherans.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Overall the religious factor worked to the advantage of Hoover, although he took no part in it.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians agree that Hoover’s national reputation and the booming economy, combined with deep splits in the Democratic Party over religion and prohibition, guaranteed his landslide victory with 58 percent of the popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Hoover’s appeal to southern white voters succeeded in cracking the “<a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">Solid South</a>”, winning the Democratic strongholds of Florida, North Carolina, Virginia, Texas and Tennessee, and nearly taking <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Alabama,_1928" title="United States presidential election in Alabama, 1928">Alabama</a> on support from <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachian</a> counties; the Deep South continued to support Smith as the Democratic candidate. This was the first time that a Republican candidate for president had carried Texas. Hoover and the national party had pursued a “lily-white southern strategy” to resuscitate the Republican Party in the South, “purging black Republicans from leadership positions in the southern wing of the G.O.P.”<sup id="cite_ref-:0_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-108">[108]</a></sup> This outraged the black leadership, which largely broke from the Republican Party, and began seeking candidates who supported <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> within the Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 1956, <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>, a leader in the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Colored_People" title="National Association for the Advancement of Colored People">NAACP</a> in the 1920s, would recall that “[i]n 1928, Negroes faced absolute dilemma. Neither Hoover nor Smith wanted the Negro vote and both publicly insulted us.”<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281929.E2.80.931933.29">Presidency (1929–1933)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Presidency (1929–1933)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Herbert_Hoover" title="Presidency of Herbert Hoover">Presidency of Herbert Hoover</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
