<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg/220px-Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg/330px-Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg/440px-Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4477" data-file-height="3628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taft_Hebert_Hoover_Oath.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inauguration of Hoover</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following his <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Herbert_Hoover" title="Inauguration of Herbert Hoover">inauguration</a>, Hoover held a press conference on his first day in office, promising a "new phase of press relations".<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-111">[111]</a></sup> He asked the group of journalists to elect a committee to recommend improvements to the White House press conference. Hoover declined to use a spokesman, instead asking reporters to directly quote him and giving them handouts with his statements ahead of time. In his first 120 days in office, he held more regular and frequent press conferences than any other President, before or since. However, he changed his press policies after the 1929 stock market crash, screening reporters and greatly reducing his availability.<sup id="cite_ref-american_chronicle_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_chronicle-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover entered office with a plan to reform the nation's regulatory system, believing that a federal bureaucracy should have limited regulation over a country's economic system.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Hoover sought a balance among labor, capital, and the government, and he has been variously labeled a <a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporatist</a> or <a href="/wiki/Associationalism" title="Associationalism">associationalist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985106_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985106-113">[113]</a></sup> Hoover saw the presidency as a vehicle for improving the conditions of all Americans by encouraging public-private cooperation—what he termed "volunteerism". He tended to oppose governmental coercion or intervention, as he thought they infringed on American ideals of individualism and self-reliance.<sup id="cite_ref-9iopl_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9iopl-114">[114]</a></sup> Hoover made extensive use of commissions to study issues and propose solutions, and many of those commissions were sponsored by private donors rather than by the government. One of the commissions started by Hoover, the Research Committee on Social Trends, was tasked with surveying the entirety of American society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200984-85_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200984-85-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Henry Hoover</a> was an activist First Lady. She typified the new woman of the post–World War I era: intelligent, robust, and aware of multiple female possibilities.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>White House physician Admiral <a href="/wiki/Joel_T._Boone" class="mw-redirect" title="Joel T. Boone">Joel T. Boone</a> invented the sport <a href="/wiki/Hooverball" title="Hooverball">Hooverball</a> to keep Hoover fit while in the White House. Hooverball is a combination of <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, played with a <span class="nowrap">6 lb</span> medicine ball. Hoover and several staff members played it each morning, earning them the nickname <i><a href="/wiki/Medicine_Ball_Cabinet" title="Medicine Ball Cabinet">Medicine Ball Cabinet</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hooverball_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hooverball-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies">Domestic policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Domestic policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover seldom mentioned civil rights while he was President. He believed that African-Americans and other races could improve themselves with education and individual initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> He opposed federal anti-lynching laws, and when lynchings occurred in the South, including one incident linked to his party's efforts to 'Republicanize' southern states, he offered only verbal condemnation.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>First Lady <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Hoover</a> defied custom and invited the wife of Republican <a href="/wiki/Oscar_De_Priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Oscar De Priest">Oscar De Priest</a>, the only African-American member in Congress, to <a href="/wiki/Jessie_De_Priest_tea_at_the_White_House" title="Jessie De Priest tea at the White House">tea at the White House</a>. <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> was the previous African-American to have dined at the White House, with <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Curtis" title="Charles Curtis">Charles Curtis</a>, the nation's first <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> Vice President, was from the <a href="/wiki/Kaw_people" title="Kaw people">Kaw tribe</a> in Kansas.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Hoover's humanitarian and Quaker reputation, along with Curtis as a vice-president, gave special meaning to his Indian policies. His Quaker upbringing influenced his views that Native Americans needed to achieve economic self-sufficiency. As President, he appointed Charles J. Rhoads as commissioner of Indian affairs. Hoover supported Rhoads' commitment to Indian assimilation and sought to minimize the federal role in Indian affairs. His goal was to have Indians acting as individuals (not as tribes) and to assume the responsibilities of citizenship granted with the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Citizenship_Act_of_1924" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Citizenship Act of 1924">Indian Citizenship Act of 1924</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Depression">Great Depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Great Depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a></div>
<p>On taking office, Hoover said that "given the chance to go forward with the policies of the last eight years, we shall soon with the help of God, be in sight of the day when poverty will be banished from this nation."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Having seen the fruits of prosperity brought by technological progress, many shared Hoover's optimism, and the already bullish stock market climbed even higher on Hoover's accession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200980-81_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200980-81-123">[123]</a></sup> But within months of taking office, the <a href="/wiki/Stock_Market_Crash_of_1929" class="mw-redirect" title="Stock Market Crash of 1929">Stock Market Crash of 1929</a> (also known as Black Tuesday) occurred, and the worldwide economy began to spiral downward into the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_Great_Depression" title="Causes of the Great Depression">causes of the Great Depression</a> remain a matter of debate,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufman2012502_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufman2012502-125">[125]</a></sup> but Hoover viewed a lack of confidence in the financial system as the fundamental economic problem facing the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHouck155-156_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHouck155-156-126">[126]</a></sup> He sought to avoid direct federal intervention, believing that the best way to bolster the economy was through the strengthening of businesses such as banks and railroads. He also feared that allowing individuals on the "<a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">dole</a>" would permanently weaken the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson350-351_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson350-351-127">[127]</a></sup> Instead, Hoover strongly believed that local governments and private giving should address the needs of individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009b_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009b-128">[128]</a></sup> A reserved man with a fear of public speaking, Hoover allowed his political enemies to define him as cold, incompetent, reactionary, and out-of-touch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998351-352_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998351-352-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_policies">Early policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Early policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hoover pursued many policies in an attempt to pull the country out of depression, while attempting to restrain the federal government from becoming directly involved in commercial affairs. In the days following Black Tuesday, Hoover gathered business and labor leaders, asking them to avoid wage cuts and work stoppages while the country faced what he believed would be a short recession similar to the <a href="/wiki/Depression_of_1920%E2%80%9321" title="Depression of 1920–21">Depression of 1920–21</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009104-105_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009104-105-130">[130]</a></sup> Some economists, such as Lee Ohanian, point to the resulting <a href="/wiki/Real_rigidity" title="Real rigidity">wage rigidity</a> as a key cause of the severity of the Great Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufman2012155-156_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufman2012155-156-131">[131]</a></sup> Hoover also authorized the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Repatriation" title="Mexican Repatriation">Mexican Repatriation</a> program to help unemployed Mexican citizens return home. The program was largely a <a href="/wiki/Forced_migration" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced migration">forced migration</a> of approximately 500,000 people to Mexico, and continued until 1937. In the spring of 1930, Hoover acquired from Congress an additional $100 million to continue the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Farm_Board" title="Federal Farm Board">Federal Farm Board</a> lending and purchasing policies. At the end of 1929, the FFB established the National Wool Marketing Corporation (NWMC), a national wool cooperative made up of 30 state associations.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Hoover also supported new public works projects, although his fear of budget deficits led him to oppose expansive projects such as that contemplated by the <a href="/wiki/Muscle_Shoals_Bill" title="Muscle Shoals Bill">Muscle Shoals Bill</a>, which sought to establish government production and distribution of power in the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley" title="Tennessee Valley">Tennessee Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_198598-99.2C_134-135_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_198598-99.2C_134-135-133">[133]</a></sup> In autumn 1930, Hoover established the <a href="/wiki/President%27s_Organization_for_Unemployment_Relief" title="President's Organization for Unemployment Relief">President's Organization for Unemployment Relief</a>, which issues press releases urging companies to hire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009b_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009b-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover had taken office hoping to raise agricultural tariffs in order to help farmers reeling from the farm crisis of the 1920s, but his attempt to raise agricultural tariffs became connected with attempts to raise tariffs for other goods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_198593-97_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_198593-97-134">[134]</a></sup> In June 1930, over the objection of many economists, Congress approved and Hoover reluctantly signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Smoot%E2%80%93Hawley_Tariff_Act" title="Smoot–Hawley Tariff Act">Smoot–Hawley Tariff Act</a>. The legislation raised tariffs on thousands of imported items. The intent of the act was to encourage the purchase of American-made products by increasing the cost of imported goods, while raising revenue for the federal government and protecting farmers. However, economic depression had spread worldwide, and Canada, France and other nations retaliated by raising tariffs on imports from the U.S. The result was to contract international trade, and worsen the Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Progressive Republicans such as Senator Borah were outraged when Hoover signed the bill, and Hoover's relations with that wing of the party never recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200991-92_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_200991-92-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_policies">Later policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Later policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For much of his presidency, Hoover opposed congressional proposals to provide federal relief, and he feared that Congress would impose a federal relief program that would infringe on the prerogatives of state and local governments and philanthropic organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985147-149_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985147-149-137">[137]</a></sup> Hoover created the National Credit Corporation, a voluntary association of bankers, but the organization did not manage to save banks or ease credit as Hoover had hoped it would.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985152-153_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985152-153-138">[138]</a></sup> As the Great Depression continued, Hoover finally heeded calls for more direct federal intervention, though he vetoed a bill that would have allowed direct federal lending to individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson_1972508-511_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson_1972508-511-139">[139]</a></sup> In January 1932, Hoover signed a bill creating the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Finance_Corporation" title="Reconstruction Finance Corporation">Reconstruction Finance Corporation</a> (RFC).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985154_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985154-140">[140]</a></sup> The RFC's initial goal was to provide government-secured loans to financial institutions, railroads, and local governments to continue relief programs. The RFC saved numerous businesses from failure, but it failed to stimulate commercial lending as Hoover had hoped, partly because it was run by conservative bankers unwilling to make riskier loans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985162-163_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985162-163-141">[141]</a></sup> The RFC would be adopted by Roosevelt and greatly expanded as part of his New Deal.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> With the RFC failing to stem the economic crisis, Hoover signed the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Relief_and_Construction_Act" title="Emergency Relief and Construction Act">Emergency Relief and Construction Act</a>, a major public works bill, in July 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985163-166_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985163-166-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout his presidency, Hoover defended the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>, and derided any other <a href="/wiki/Monetary_system" title="Monetary system">monetary system</a> as "collectivism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEichengreenTemin2000196-197_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEichengreenTemin2000196-197-144">[144]</a></sup> Hoover and Senator <a href="/wiki/Carter_Glass" title="Carter Glass">Carter Glass</a>, another gold standard proponent, recognized that they needed to stop <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a> by encouraging the lending of credit. Hoover was instrumental in passing the <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass–Steagall Act">Glass–Steagall Act</a> of 1932, which allowed for prime rediscounting at the Federal Reserve, in turn allowing further inflation of credit and bank reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERappleye2016312-314_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERappleye2016312-314-145">[145]</a></sup> In July 1932, Hoover signed the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Home_Loan_Bank_Act" title="Federal Home Loan Bank Act">Federal Home Loan Bank Act</a>, establishing 12 district banks overseen by a Federal Home Loan Bank Board in a manner similar to the Federal Reserve System.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERappleye2016309_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERappleye2016309-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1930, unemployment stood at 8.9%, and many assumed that the United States was just in another recession.<sup id="cite_ref-rsamuelson1_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rsamuelson1-147">[147]</a></sup> But by 1932, unemployment had reached 24.9%, businesses had defaulted on record numbers of loans, and more than 5,000 banks had failed, especially small rural banks.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> The homeless lived in shantytowns they called <a href="/wiki/Hooverville" title="Hooverville">Hoovervilles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DicksonAllen2010_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DicksonAllen2010-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/220px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/330px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg/440px-Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="372" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_and_Ted_Joslin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover in the Oval Office with Ted Joslin, 1932</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxes.2C_revenues.2C_and_deficits">Taxes, revenues, and deficits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Taxes, revenues, and deficits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Debt1929-50.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/220px-Debt1929-50.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/330px-Debt1929-50.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Debt1929-50.jpg/440px-Debt1929-50.jpg 2x" data-file-width="651" data-file-height="440" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Debt1929-50.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
National debt as a fraction of GNP up from 20% to 40% under Hoover. From <i>Historical Statistics US</i> (1976)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover was a firm believer in balanced budgets, and sought to avoid a budget deficit by greatly increasing tax rates on the wealthy. To pay for government programs and to make up for revenue lost due to the Depression, Hoover signed the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1932" title="Revenue Act of 1932">Revenue Act of 1932</a>. The act increased taxes across the board, so that top earners were taxed at 63% on their net income - up from 25% when Herbert Hoover took office. The 1932 Act also increased the tax on the net income of corporations from 12% to 13.75%.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Additionally, under Hoover, the <a href="/wiki/Estate_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Estate tax">estate tax</a> was doubled and <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax" title="Corporate tax">corporate taxes</a> were raised by almost 15%. Also, a "check tax" took effect, placing a 2-cent tax (over 30 cents in today's economy) on all bank checks. Economists William D. Lastrapes and George Selgin conclude that the check tax was "an important contributing factor to that period's severe monetary contraction".<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Despite the passage of the Revenue Act, the federal government continued to run a budget deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985161_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985161-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/170px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/255px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg/340px-President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1182" data-file-height="1404" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_%26_Mrs_Hoover_in_Belvidere_IL_cph.3b12319.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert and Lou Henry Hoover aboard a train in Illinois.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Leuchtenberg, Hoover was "the last American president to take office with no conspicuous need to pay attention to the rest of the world." But during Hoover's term, the world order established with the 1919 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> began to crumble.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009117_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009117-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>As president, Hoover largely made good on his pledge made prior to assuming office not to interfere in Latin America's internal affairs. In 1930, he released the <a href="/wiki/Clark_Memorandum" title="Clark Memorandum">Clark Memorandum</a>, a rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Corollary" title="Roosevelt Corollary">Roosevelt Corollary</a> and a move towards non-interventionism in Latin America. Hoover did not completely refrain from the use of the military in Latin American affairs; he thrice threatened intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, and he sent warships to <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> to support the government against a left-wing revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009120-121_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009120-121-154">[154]</a></sup> But he wound down the <a href="/wiki/Banana_Wars" title="Banana Wars">Banana Wars</a>, ending the <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Nicaragua" title="United States occupation of Nicaragua">occupation of Nicaragua</a> and nearly bringing an end to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Haiti" title="United States occupation of Haiti">occupation of Haiti</a>. Franklin Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/Good_Neighbor_policy" title="Good Neighbor policy">Good Neighbor policy</a> would continue the trend towards non-interventionism in Latin America. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985183-186_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985183-186-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the United States remained outside of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, Hoover showed a willingness to work within multilateral structures. Hoover pursued United States membership in the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a>, but the Senate never voted on his proposal. The Senate also defeated Hoover's proposed <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a> Treaty with Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27BrienRosen198192_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27BrienRosen198192-156">[156]</a></sup> In 1930, the United States and other major naval powers signed the <a href="/wiki/London_Naval_Treaty" title="London Naval Treaty">London Naval Treaty</a>, an extension of the 1922 <a href="/wiki/Washington_Naval_Treaty" title="Washington Naval Treaty">Washington Naval Treaty</a>, which sought to prevent a naval <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985175-176_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985175-176-157">[157]</a></sup> The treaty represented the first time that the naval powers had agreed to cap their tonnage of auxiliary vessels (previous agreements had focused on <a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">capital ships</a>), but the treaty failed to include France or Italy. The treaty provoked a nationalist backlash in Japan due to its reconfirmation of the "5-5-3" ratio which limited Japan to a smaller fleet than the United States or the United Kingdom. At the 1932 <a href="/wiki/World_Disarmament_Conference" title="World Disarmament Conference">World Disarmament Conference</a>, Hoover urged worldwide cutbacks in armaments and the outlawing of <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tanks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bomber" title="Bomber">bombers</a>, but his proposals were not adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009117-119_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009117-119-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1931, Japan <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Japanese invasion of Manchuria">invaded</a> <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, defeating the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%9349)" title="Republic of China (1912–49)">Republic of China</a>'s military forces and establishing <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a>, a puppet state. The Hoover administration deplored the invasion, but also sought to avoid antagonizing the Japanese, fearing that taking too strong of a stand would weaken the moderate forces in the Japanese government. In response to the Japanese invasion, Hoover and Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stimson" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Stimson">Henry Stimson</a> outlined the <a href="/wiki/Stimson_Doctrine" title="Stimson Doctrine">Stimson Doctrine</a>, which held that the United States would not recognize territories gained by force. The Hoover administration based this declaration on the 1928 <a href="/wiki/Kellogg-Briand_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Kellogg-Briand Pact">Kellogg-Briand Pact</a>, in which several nations (including Japan and the United States) renounced war and promised to peacefully solve disputes. In the aftermath of invasion of Manchuria, Stimson and other members of the Cabinet came to believe that war with Japan might be inevitable, though Hoover continued to push for <a href="/wiki/Disarmament" title="Disarmament">disarmament</a> among the world powers.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1931, Hoover issued the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Moratorium" title="Hoover Moratorium">Hoover Moratorium</a>, calling for a one-year halt in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_reparations" title="World War I reparations">reparation</a> payments. Hoover also made U.S. bankers agree to refrain from demanding payment on private loans from Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009126-127_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009126-127-160">[160]</a></sup> Hoover hoped that the moratorium would help stabilize the European economy, which he viewed as a major cause of economic troubles in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985143-144_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985143-144-161">[161]</a></sup> As the moratorium neared its expiration the following year, an attempt to find a permanent solution was made at the <a href="/wiki/Lausanne_Conference_of_1932" title="Lausanne Conference of 1932">Lausanne Conference of 1932</a>. A working compromise was never established, and reparations payments virtually stopped.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonus_Army">Bonus Army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Bonus Army">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bonus_Army" title="Bonus Army">Bonus Army</a></div>
<p>Thousands of World War I veterans and their families demonstrated and camped out in Washington, DC, during June 1932, calling for immediate payment of a bonus that had been promised by the <a href="/wiki/World_War_Adjusted_Compensation_Act" title="World War Adjusted Compensation Act">World War Adjusted Compensation Act</a> in 1924 for payment in 1945. Although offered money by <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congress</a> to return home, some members of the "Bonus Army" remained. Washington police attempted to remove the demonstrators from their camp, but they were outnumbered and unsuccessful. Shots were fired by the police in a futile attempt to attain order, and two protesters were killed while many officers were injured. Hoover sent U.S. Army forces led by General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> and helped by lower ranking officers <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> to stop a march. MacArthur, believing he was fighting a communist revolution, chose to clear out the camp with military force. In the ensuing clash, hundreds of civilians were injured. Hoover had sent orders that the Army was not to move on the encampment, but MacArthur chose to ignore the command. Hoover was incensed, but refused to reprimand MacArthur. The entire incident was another devastating negative for Hoover in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1932" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidential election, 1932">1932</a> election. That led New York governor and Democratic presidential candidate Franklin Roosevelt to declare of Hoover: "There is nothing inside the man but jelly!"</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1932_re-election_campaign">1932 re-election campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 1932 re-election campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:179px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Campaign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Hoover_Campaign.jpg" width="177" height="215" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="177" data-file-height="215" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Campaign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover addresses a large crowd in his 1932 campaign.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">United States presidential election, 1932</a></div>
<p>Despite the economic calamity facing the nation and his dim hopes for re-election, Hoover faced little opposition for re-nomination at the <a href="/wiki/1932_Republican_National_Convention" title="1932 Republican National Convention">1932 Republican National Convention</a>. Some Republicans talked of nominating Coolidge, former Vice President <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dawes" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Dawes">Charles Dawes</a>, or Senator <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Johnson" title="Hiram Johnson">Hiram Johnson</a>, but all passed on the opportunity to challenge Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985194-195_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985194-195-163">[163]</a></sup> Curtis was re-nominated as Hoover's running mate. Franklin D. Roosevelt won the presidential nomination on the third ballot of the <a href="/wiki/1932_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1932 Democratic National Convention">1932 Democratic National Convention</a>, defeating the 1928 Democratic nominee, <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith" title="Al Smith">Al Smith</a>. Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a> was nominated as Roosevelt's running mate. By 1932, the <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> was in 12 million homes, changing the nature of presidential campaigns. No longer could presidents change the content of their speeches for each audience; anyone with a radio could listen to every major speech.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998349_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998349-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover originally planned to make only one or two major speeches, and to leave the rest of the campaigning to proxies, as sitting presidents had traditionally done. However, encouraged by Republican pleas and outraged by Democratic claims, Hoover entered the public fray. In his nine major radio addresses Hoover primarily defended his administration and his philosophy of government. Hoover urged voters to hold to the "foundations of experience," rejecting the notion that government interventionism could save the country from the Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998359_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998359-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1932.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/ElectoralCollege1932.svg/275px-ElectoralCollege1932.svg.png" width="275" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/ElectoralCollege1932.svg/413px-ElectoralCollege1932.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/ElectoralCollege1932.svg/550px-ElectoralCollege1932.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1932.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1932 electoral vote results.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his campaign trips around the country, Hoover was faced with perhaps the most hostile crowds of any sitting president. Besides having his train and motorcades pelted with eggs and rotten fruit, he was often heckled while speaking, and on several occasions, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> halted attempts to kill Hoover by disgruntled citizens, including capturing one man nearing Hoover carrying sticks of dynamite, and another already having removed several spikes from the rails in front of the President's train.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democrats attacked Hoover as the cause of the Great Depression, and for being indifferent to the suffering of millions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998353_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998353-167">[167]</a></sup> As Governor of New York, Roosevelt had called on the New York legislature to provide aid for the needy, establishing Roosevelt's reputation for being more favorable toward government interventionism during the economic crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009138-140_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009138-140-168">[168]</a></sup> Fausold rejects the notion that the two nominees were similar ideologically, pointing to differences between the two on federal spending on public works, agricultural issues, Prohibition, and the tariff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985206-208_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985206-208-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover's attempts to vindicate his administration fell on deaf ears, as much of the public blamed his administration for the depression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998361-362_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarcasson_1998361-362-170">[170]</a></sup> Roosevelt won 57.4 percent of the popular vote compared to Hoover's 39.7%. Hoover’s popular vote was reduced by nineteen percent from his result in the 1928 election, and he carried just five <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern states</a> and Delaware. Roosevelt became the first Democratic presidential nominee to win a <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_presidential_elections_by_popular_vote_margin" title="List of United States presidential elections by popular vote margin">majority of the popular vote</a> since the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009142_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009142-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/170px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/255px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg/340px-FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="3480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FDR_Inauguration_1933.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover with Franklin D. Roosevelt, March 4, 1933</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hoover departed from Washington in March 1933, bitter at his election loss and continuing unpopularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-149_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-149-172">[172]</a></sup> Upon leaving office, Hoover was the only living ex-President for nearly 19 years, until <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> left office in 1953. The Hoovers went first to New York City, where they stayed for a while in the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_Astoria_New_York" title="Waldorf Astoria New York">Waldorf Astoria hotel</a>. Later that spring, they returned to California to their Stanford residence. Hoover enjoyed returning to the men's clubs that he had long been involved with, including the <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Club" title="Bohemian Club">Bohemian Club</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific-Union_Club" title="Pacific-Union Club">Pacific-Union Club</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_Club_of_San_Francisco" title="University Club of San Francisco">University Club</a> in San Francisco.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Hoover and his wife lived in Palo Alto until her death in 1944, at which point Hoover began to live permanently at the Waldorf Astoria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009155-156_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009155-156-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover liked to drive his car, accompanied by his wife or a friend (former Presidents did not get <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> protection until the 1960s), and drive on wandering journeys, visiting Western mining camps or small towns where he often went unrecognized, or heading up to the mountains, or deep into the woods, to go fishing in relative solitude. A year before his death, his own fishing days behind him, he published <i>Fishing For Fun—And To Wash Your Soul</i>, the last of more than sixteen books in his lifetime.</p>
<p>In 1939, Hoover became the first Honorary Chairman of <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy_Foundation" title="Tolstoy Foundation">Tolstoy Foundation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Valley_Cottage,_New_York" title="Valley Cottage, New York">Valley Cottage, New York</a>, and served in this capacity until his death in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/250px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" width="250" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/375px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2c/Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg/500px-Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="430" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Books_fishing_for_fun.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover's book, <i>Fishing For Fun – And To Wash Your Soul</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_Roosevelt">Opposition to Roosevelt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Opposition to Roosevelt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover continued to closely follow national events after his retirement, becoming a constant critic of Franklin Roosevelt. In response to continued attacks on his character and presidency, Hoover wrote more than two dozen books, including <i>The Challenge to Liberty</i> (1934), which harshly criticized the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. Hoover feared that the country had surrendered its "freedom of mind and spirit" to the New Deal. He described the <a href="/wiki/National_Recovery_Administration" title="National Recovery Administration">National Recovery Administration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Adjustment_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Agricultural Adjustment Administration">Agricultural Adjustment Administration</a> as "fascistic," and the <a href="/wiki/1933_Banking_Act" title="1933 Banking Act">1933 Banking Act</a> as a "move to gigantic socialism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-151_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-151-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._(no_original_caption)_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg/200px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg" width="200" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg/300px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg/400px-President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._%28no_original_caption%29_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Herbert_Hoover_with_a_day%27s_catch._(no_original_caption)_-_NARA_-_513114.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover fishing in his home state of Iowa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Only 58 when he left office, Hoover held out hope for another term during the 1930s. At the <a href="/wiki/1936_Republican_National_Convention" title="1936 Republican National Convention">1936 Republican National Convention</a>, Hoover's speech attacking the New Deal was well received, but the nomination went to Kansas Governor <a href="/wiki/Alf_Landon" title="Alf Landon">Alf Landon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009151-153_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009151-153-177">[177]</a></sup> In the general election, Hoover campaigned for Landon's unsuccessful campaign with numerous well-publicized speeches attacking New Deal liberalism.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Though Hoover was eager to oppose Roosevelt at every turn, and Senator <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Vandenberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Vandenberg">Arthur Vandenberg</a> and other Republicans urged the still-unpopular Hoover to remain out of the fray during the debate over Roosevelt's proposed <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Reorganization_Bill_of_1937" class="mw-redirect" title="Judiciary Reorganization Bill of 1937">Judiciary Reorganization Bill of 1937</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/1940_Republican_National_Convention" title="1940 Republican National Convention">1940 Republican National Convention</a>, Hoover again hoped for the presidential nomination, and was dismayed when it went to the internationalist <a href="/wiki/Wendell_Willkie" title="Wendell Willkie">Wendell Willkie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-154_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009147-154-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover remained popular in Europe, and he was honored in France and Belgium. During a 1938 trip to Europe, Hoover met with <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and stayed at <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a>'s hunting lodge. Hoover expressed dismay at the persecution of Jews in Germany, but believed that Hitler did not present a threat. Instead, Hoover believed that Roosevelt posed the biggest threat to peace, as he believed that Roosevelt discouraged France and the United Kingdom from reaching an "accommodation" with Germany. After the September 1939 <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> by Germany, Hoover opposed United States intervention in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Lend-Lease" title="Lend-Lease">Lend-Lease</a> policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009152-154_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009152-154-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hoover became a vocal supporter of providing relief to countries in <a href="/wiki/Nazi-occupied_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi-occupied Europe">Nazi-occupied Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walch2003_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walch2003-181">[181]</a></sup> He was instrumental in creating the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Polish_Relief" title="Commission for Polish Relief">Commission for Polish Relief</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Relief_Fund" title="Finnish Relief Fund">Finnish Relief Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hoover1964_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoover1964-183">[183]</a></sup> In 1939, Roosevelt asked Hoover to the White House for advice on getting aid to Poland, but Hoover turned down the offer. Much to his own frustration, Hoover was not called upon to serve after the <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_States_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of the United States during World War II">United States entered World War II</a> due to his differences with Roosevelt and his continuing unpopularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009155-156_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009155-156-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>During a radio broadcast on June 29, 1941, one week after the Nazi invasion of the Soviet Union, Hoover disparaged any "tacit alliance" between the U.S. and the USSR by saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If we go further and join the war and we win, then we have won for Stalin the grip of communism on Russia... Again I say, if we join the war and Stalin wins, we have aided him to impose more communism on Europe and the world. At least we could not with such a bedfellow say to our sons that by making the supreme sacrifice, they are restoring freedom to the world. War alongside Stalin to impose freedom is more than a travesty. It is a tragedy.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post.E2.80.93World_War_II">Post–World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Post–World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/220px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/330px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg/440px-Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="745" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_men_Allan_Herbert_Sr_Andrew_1950.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Hoover with his son <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hoover" title="Allan Hoover">Allan</a> <i>(left)</i> and his grandson Andrew <i>(above)</i>, 1950</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Hoover became friends with President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> despite their ideological differences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009157-158_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009157-158-185">[185]</a></sup> Hoover joked that they were for many years the sole members of the <a href="/wiki/Lifespan_timeline_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Lifespan timeline of Presidents of the United States">"trade union" of former Presidents</a>. Because of Hoover's experience with Germany at the end of World War I, in 1946 President Truman selected the former president to tour Germany to ascertain the food needs of the occupied nation. Hoover toured what was to become West Germany in <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a>'s old train coach and produced <a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Economic_Mission_to_Germany_and_Austria" title="The President's Economic Mission to Germany and Austria">a number of reports</a> critical of U.S. occupation policy. The economy of Germany had "sunk to the lowest level in a hundred years".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> He stated in one report:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is the illusion that the New Germany left after the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Eastern_Germany#Potsdam_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical Eastern Germany">annexations</a> can be reduced to a "<a href="/wiki/Morgenthau_Plan" title="Morgenthau Plan">pastoral state</a>". It cannot be done unless we <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">exterminate</a> or move 25,000,000 people out of it.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External audio</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.loc.gov/rr/record/pressclub/hoover.html">National Press Club Luncheon Speakers</a>, Herbert Hoover, March 10, 1954, 37:23, Hoover speaks starting at 7:25 about the second reorganization commission, <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a><sup id="cite_ref-loc_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc-188">[188]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On Hoover's initiative, a school meals program in the American and British occupation zones of Germany was begun on April 14, 1947. The program served 3,500,000 children aged six through 18. A total of 40,000 tons of American food was provided during the <i>Hooverspeisung</i> (Hoover meals).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1947, Truman appointed Hoover to a commission, which elected him chairman, to reorganize the executive departments. This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Commission" title="Hoover Commission">Hoover Commission</a>. Led by Hoover, the commission recommended changes designed to strengthen the president's ability to manage the federal government. Though Hoover had opposed FDR's concentration of power in the 1930s, he believed that a stronger presidency was required with the advent of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Age" title="Atomic Age">Atomic Age</a>. In 1953, Hoover was appointed to a similar commission by President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>. Despite the appointment, Hoover disliked Eisenhower, faulting the latter's failure to roll back the New Deal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009158-159_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeuchtenberg_2009158-159-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hhoover.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/220px-Hhoover.jpeg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/330px-Hhoover.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Hhoover.jpeg/440px-Hhoover.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2376" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hhoover.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover's official <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> portrait painted by Elmer Wesley Greene.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his advancing years, Hoover continued to work nearly full-time both on writing (among his literary works is <i>The Ordeal of Woodrow Wilson</i>, a bestseller, and the first time one former President had ever written a biography about another), as well as overseeing the Hoover Institution at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, which housed not only his own professional papers, but also those of a number of other former high ranking governmental and military servants. He also threw himself into fund-raising for the Boys Clubs (now the <a href="/wiki/Boys_%26_Girls_Clubs_of_America" title="Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America">Boys &amp; Girls Clubs of America</a>), which became his pet charity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1958, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Former_Presidents_Act" title="Former Presidents Act">Former Presidents Act</a>, offering a $25,000 yearly pension to each former president.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_2008_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2008-190">[190]</a></sup> Hoover, who was the only other living former president, took the pension even though he did not need the money; reportedly, he did so to avoid embarrassing former President Truman whose precarious financial status played a role in the law's enactment.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Final years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hoover was the only living former Republican president between his last day in office in 1933 and Eisenhower's last day in office in 1961. Starting with the 1948 convention, Hoover was feted as the guest of "farewell" ceremonies, with the unspoken assumption that the aging former President might not survive until the next convention. In 1960, Hoover appeared at his final Republican National Convention. Joking to the delegates, he said, "Apparently, my last three good-byes didn't take." Although he lived to see the 1964 convention, ill health prevented him from attending, and his absence was acknowledged in presidential nominee <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a>'s acceptance speech. In 1962, Hoover had a malignant intestinal tumor removed. Ten months later he had severe gastrointestinal bleeding and seemed terminally ill and frail, but his mind was clear and he maintained a great deal of correspondence. Although the illness would get worse over time, he refused to be hospitalized.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><span id="Death"></span> Hoover died following massive <a href="/wiki/Internal_bleeding" title="Internal bleeding">internal bleeding</a> at the age of 90 in his New York City suite at 11:35&#160;a.m. on October 20, 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> 31 years, seven months, and sixteen days after leaving office. At the time of his death, he had the <a href="/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_the_United_States_by_age" class="mw-redirect" title="List of presidents of the United States by age">longest retirement</a> of any President.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Former President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> surpassed the length of Hoover's retirement on September 7, 2012. At the time of Hoover's death he was the second longest-lived president after <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>; both were since surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>. He had outlived by 20 years his wife, Lou Henry Hoover, who had died in 1944, and he was the last living member of both the Harding and <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge#Administration_and_Cabinet" title="Calvin Coolidge">Coolidge</a> administrations.</p>
<p>By the time of his death, he had rehabilitated his image.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985244_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFausold_1985244-194">[194]</a></sup> His birthplace in Iowa and an Oregon home where he lived as a child, became National Landmarks during his lifetime. His <a href="/wiki/Rapidan_Camp" title="Rapidan Camp">Rapidan fishing camp</a> in Virginia, which he had donated to the government in 1933, is now a National Historic Landmark within the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_National_Park" title="Shenandoah National Park">Shenandoah National Park</a>. Hoover and his wife are buried at the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_and_Museum" title="Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum">Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch, Iowa</a>. Hoover was honored with a <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a>, the last of three in a span of 12 months, coming as it did just after the deaths of President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>. Former <a href="/wiki/Chaplain_of_the_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaplain of the Senate">Chaplain of the Senate</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Brown_Harris" title="Frederick Brown Harris">Frederick Brown Harris</a> officiated. All three had two things in common: the commanding general of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Military_District_of_Washington" title="United States Army Military District of Washington">Military District of Washington</a> during those funerals was Army Major General <a href="/wiki/Philip_C._Wehle" title="Philip C. Wehle">Philip C. Wehle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Riderless_horse" title="Riderless horse">riderless horse</a> was <a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(horse)" title="Black Jack (horse)">Black Jack</a>, who also served in that role during <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>'s funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1912 Hoover published the first English edition of the medieval mining compendium <i><a href="/wiki/De_Re_Metallica" class="mw-redirect" title="De Re Metallica">De Re Metallica</a></i> (<i>On the Nature of Metals</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> It was translated from Latin by himself and his wife, who was a geologist and proficient in Latin. It remains the standard English translation.</p>
<p>Hoover began his magnum opus <i>Freedom Betrayed</i><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> in 1944 as part of a proposed autobiography. This turned into a significant work critiquing the foreign policy of the United States during the period from the 1930s to 1945. Essentially an attack on the statesmanship of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, Hoover completed this work in his 90th year but it was not published until the historian <a href="/wiki/George_H._Nash" title="George H. Nash">George H. Nash</a> took on the task of editing it. Significant themes are his belief that the western democratic powers should have let Nazi Germany and Soviet Russia assail and weaken each other, and opposition to the British guarantee of Poland's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Other works include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.gutenberg.org/files/26697/26697-h/26697-h.htm">Principles of Mining</a> (1909)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/foodguideforwars14055gut">Food Guide for War Service at Home Prepared under the direction of the United States Food Administration</a> (1918)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/cu31924013849330">Preface to a report of the United States Food Administration</a> (1920)</li>
<li>American Individualism (1922)</li>
<li>The Challenge to Liberty (1935)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/dudeman5685_yahoo_AIND">American Ideals Versus the New Deal</a> (1936)</li>
<li>The Problems Of Lasting Peace (1942)</li>
<li>Prefaces to Peace (1943)</li>
<li>The Memoirs Of Herbert Hoover: 1874 - 1920 Years Of Adventure (1951)</li>
<li>The Memoirs Of Herbert Hoover: The Cabinet and the Presidency, 1920-1933 (1952)</li>
<li>The Memoirs Of Herbert Hoover: 1929 - 1941 the Great Depression (1952)</li>
<li>The Ordeal of Woodrow Wilson (1958)</li>
<li>On Growing Up: His Letters from and to American Children (1962)</li>
<li>Fishing for Fun: And to Wash Your Soul (1963)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage_and_memorials">Heritage and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Heritage and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/220px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/330px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg/440px-Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="633" data-file-height="270" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library_003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hoover Presidential Library</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/220px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/330px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG/440px-Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="842" data-file-height="1054" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Plaque_Poznan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plaque in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a> honoring Herbert Hoover</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_Presidential_Library-Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert Hoover Presidential Library-Museum">Herbert Hoover Presidential Library and Museum</a> is located in <a href="/wiki/West_Branch,_Iowa" title="West Branch, Iowa">West Branch, Iowa</a> next to the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover_National_Historic_Site" title="Herbert Hoover National Historic Site">Herbert Hoover National Historic Site</a>. The library is one of thirteen presidential libraries run by the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives and Records Administration</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hoover%E2%80%93Minthorn_House" title="Hoover–Minthorn House">Hoover-Minthorn House</a>, where Hoover lived from 1885 to 1891, is located in <a href="/wiki/Newberg,_Oregon" title="Newberg, Oregon">Newberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_and_Herbert_Hoover_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House">Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House</a>, built in 1919 in <a href="/wiki/Stanford,_California" title="Stanford, California">Stanford, California</a>, is now the official residence of the president of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>. Also located at Stanford is the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>, a think tank and research institution started by Hoover.</p>
<p>Hoover's rustic rural presidential retreat, Rapidan Camp (also known as Camp Hoover) in the <a href="/wiki/Shenandoah_National_Park" title="Shenandoah National Park">Shenandoah National Park</a>, Virginia, has been restored and opened to the public. The <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Dam" title="Hoover Dam">Hoover Dam</a> is named in his honor, as are numerous <a href="/wiki/Elementary_school" title="Elementary school">elementary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">middle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/High_school" class="mw-redirect" title="High school">high schools</a> across the United States.</p>
<p>On December 10, 2008, Hoover's great-granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Hoover" title="Margaret Hoover">Margaret Hoover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Senate of Puerto Rico">Senate of Puerto Rico</a> President <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_McClintock" title="Kenneth McClintock">Kenneth McClintock</a> unveiled a life-sized bronze statue of Hoover at Puerto Rico's Territorial Capitol. The statue is one of seven honoring Presidents who have visited the United States territory during their term of office.</p>
<p>One line in the <i><a href="/wiki/All_in_the_Family" title="All in the Family">All in the Family</a></i> theme song—an ironic exercise in pre–New Deal nostalgia—says "Mister, we could use a man like Herbert Hoover again".</p>
<p>The Belgian city of <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Leuven</a> named a square in the city center after Hoover, honoring him for his work as chairman of the "Commission for Relief in Belgium" during World War I. The square is near the Central Library of the Catholic University of Leuven, where a bust of the president can be seen.</p>
<p>The Polish capital of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> also has a square named after Hoover alongside the Royal Route leading to the Old Town.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Burroughs_Torrey" title="George Burroughs Torrey">George Burroughs Torrey</a> painted a portrait of him.</p>
<p>The historic townsite of <a href="/wiki/Gwalia,_Western_Australia" title="Gwalia, Western Australia">Gwalia, Western Australia</a> contains the Sons of Gwalia Museum and the Hoover House Bed and Breakfast, the renovated and restored Mining Engineers residence that was the original residence of Herbert Hoover and where he stayed in subsequent visits to the mine during the first decade of the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Herbert_Hoover&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center">
